==PAGE:3==
Abstract
The discipline of sensory analysis involves statistical analyses of sensory data to eval-
uate and conduct inference about consumer products, and it is therefore a highly
discussed subject. Sensory data consists of product scores, which are based on sen-
sory evaluations of consumer products performed by a panel of human assessors. The
purposes of sensory analyses are numerous, but our primary objective in this project
is to draw inference about product dierences.
One classic approach for the analysis of sensory data is to employ a mixed two-way
ANOVA model containing the panelist and product eect as well as the panelist-by-
product interaction. [Brockho et al., 2015] considers a multiplicative mixed model,
as an alternative to the mixed two-way ANOVA model, which diers from this classic
approach by partitioning the panelist-by-product interaction into a panelist depen-
dent scaling eect and a disagreement eect, allowing us to distinguish between these
two sources of variation. To circumvent the non-linearity of the multiplicative mixed
model, [Brockho et al., 2015] introduces the approximate linear Mixed Assessor Model
(MAM).
One key aim of this project is to handle the genuine multiplicative mixed model by a full
likelihood approach, for what reason we employ the Laplace method for the approxima-
tion of the likelihood function. In this regard, we present two implementations of the
Laplace method. The rst implementation is straightforward but inecient, and the
second makes use of the newly developed Template Model Builder (TMB) R-package,
which proved to be superior in terms of computation time. The study eventually leads
to a comparison of the approximate MAM and the multiplicative mixed model by
examining the dierences in parameter estimates and statistical inference using both
real and simulated data.
According to our ndings, the two models do not always lead to the same conclu-
sion about the product contrasts. In the simulation studies, the novel method pro-
posed in [Brockho et al., 2015] produced very appropriate 95%-condence intervals
for the product contrasts, with coverage probabilities close to 0.95. The full likeli-
hood approach provides us with prole likelihood based condence intervals, but this
method most often resulted in coverage probabilities below 0.95, which appeared to
be a consequence of the downward bias in the maximum likelihood estimated variance
components. Our study showed that condence intervals with satisfactory coverage
probabilities can be obtained by a reasonable increase in the number of observations
per random eect, decreasing the bias and the uncertainty in the estimated variance
components.
==PAGE:4==

==PAGE:5==
Resumé
Statistisk analyse af sensorisk data er en nødvendighed for at kunne drage inferens
om forbrugsprodukter, og det er derfor et omdiskuteret emne. Sensorisk data består
af point givet af smagsdommere til forbrugsprodukter på baggrund af sensoriske eval-
ueringer af produkterne. Sensorisk analyse kan have ere formål, men det primære
fokus i dette projekt er den statistiske inferens om produktforskelle.
En klassisk metode til at analysere sensorisk data er at anvende en mixed to-vejs
ANOVA model, der indeholder eekten af produkt, smagsdommer og interaktionen
mellem disse. Som et alternativ til dette, betragter [Brockho et al., 2015] en multi-
plikativ mixed model, som adskiller sig fra den klassiske to-vejs ANOVA model ved
at splitte interaktionen op i en dommerafhængig skaleringsefekt og eekten af pro-
duktuenighed mellem dommerne, hvilket gør det muligt at skelne mellem disse to
kilder til variation. For at undgå at håndtere ulineariteten i denne model, introduc-
erer [Brockho et al., 2015] i stedet "the Mixed Assessor Model" (MAM), som er en
lineær approksimation til den multiplikative mixede model.
I dette projekt håndteres den multiplikative mixede model på dens oprindelige form
vha. en likelihood-baseret metode, hvor Laplace approksimationen til log-likelihood'en
maksimeres. Denne metode implementeres indledningsvist, hvor fremgangsmetoden er
ligefrem og intuitiv, men også krævende mht. beregningstid. Hernæst implementeres
den via den nyudviklede R-pakke "Template Model Builder" (TMB), som viser sig at
have en ekstrem høj beregningshastighed.
Den statistiske inferens, der opnås ved at anvende MAM og den mixede multipliaktive
model på sensorisk data, sammenlignes, og her viser det sig at de to modeller i visse til-
fælde drager forskellige konklusioner omkring produktkontraster. Endvidere benyttes
simulationsstudier til at teste de estimerede 95%-kondensintervaller for produktkon-
trasterne. Disse studier viser, at den nye og alternative metode, foreslået i [Brockho
et al., 2015], producerer fornuftige kondensintervaller med dækningssandsynligheder
tæt på 0,95.
Via den likelihood-baserede metode er det simpelt at estimere kon-
densintervaller vha. prol likelihood'er, men denne metode resulterer oftest i dækn-
ingssandsynligheder, der er lavere end 0,95. Dette skyldes højst sandsynligt, at maksi-
mum likelihood estimerede variansparametre er biased nedad. Det viser sig dog, at det
er muligt at opnå kondensintervaller med tilfredsstillende dækningssandsynligheder
ved en realistisk øgning i antallet af observationer pr. random eect, da dette får bias
og usikkerheden på de estimerede variansparametre til at aftage.

Preface
This master thesis was prepared at the department of Applied Mathematics and Com-
puter Science at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU Compute) in fullment of
the requirements for acquiring an M.Sc. in Mathematical Modelling and Computation.
The thesis represents a workload of 35 ECTS points for both students.
The inspiration for the topic of this thesis comes from [Brockho et al., 2015] where
the mixed assessor model is introduced as a linear approximation to the more prop-
erly specied non-linear multiplicative mixed model for the analysis of sensory and
consumer data. The article states:
"No doubt, the multiplicative mixed model could be handled by likelihood meth-
ods using techniques developed for non-linear mixed models. This is not the
aim in this paper however, instead we provide a simple linear model approxi-
mation of this,..."
The aim of this thesis is to investigate the potential improvements that could be
obtained by the full likelihood approach. All code is developed in the statical pro-
gramming environment, R.
The supervisors of the project are Professor Per Bruun Brockho and Senior Re-
searcher Kasper Kristensen from DTU Compute.

Chapter 1
Introduction
Sensory analysis is a scientic discipline that involves statistical analyses of sensory
data to evaluate consumer products. Sensory data consists of product scores, which
are quantied sensory evaluations (evaluations by taste, touch, sight, smell and hear-
ing) of products made by a panel of (trained or untrained) human assessors based
on descriptive attributes of the products, such as appearance, touch, odor, texture,
temperature and taste. The purposes of sensory analyses are of course manifold, but
our primary interest lies in the product dierences also known as contrasts.
One classic approach to the analysis of sensory data is to employ a linear two-way
ANOVA model containing the panelist and product eect and the panelist-by-product
interaction. One pitfall of using the conventional panelist-by-product interaction is its
inability to distinguish between scale range dierences and the actual product disagree-
ment amongst the panelists. The scale range dierences, or simply put, the dierent
use of scale, is an interactive eect that emerge from larger separation of products by
some panelists.
In [Brockho et al., 2015] a non-linear mixed model, more specically a multiplicative
mixed model, is considered and discussed for the analysis of sensory data. The multi-
plicative mixed model diers from the classic two-way ANOVA model by partitioning
the panelist-by-product interaction into a panelist dependent scaling eect and a dis-
agreement eect, allowing us to distinguish between these two sources of variation.
The scaling eect enters the model as a panelist dependent regression coecient on
the centered products eects, thus making the model non-linear.
Brockho et al. [2015] suggests an approximate linear approach to handle the non-
linearity of the multiplicative mixed model based on the Mixed Assessor Model (MAM),
a generalization of the so-called assessor-model approach introduced in [Brockho and
Skovgaard, 1994]. An important benet of this approximate approach is the immense
simplication of theory and computations with only a modest change in interpretation.
One considerable shortcoming of the Mixed Assessor Model, however, is its inability to
produce valid condence limits for the product contrasts. Excessive adjustments are
==PAGE:14==
2
Introduction
therefore required to obtain condence limits that more closely emulate the behavior
of those obtained for the multiplicative mixed model.
One key aim of the project is to consider the multiplicative mixed model discussed in
[Brockho et al., 2015] in its genuine form and to handle this more properly specied
model by a full likelihood approach using the Laplace method. The objective is to
eciently implement the Laplace method, to uncover the benets and limitations of
a full likelihood approach and to consider key concepts such as model identiability,
model assumptions and model interpretation.
We present two ways to implement the Laplace method: 1) an intuitive and exi-
ble implementation in the programming environment, R, suggested by [Christensen
and Brockho, 2012], 2) a simple and quick implementation using Automatic Dier-
entiation (AD) via the Template Model Builder (TMB), an open source R-package
[Kristensen, 2014], which immensely reduces the overall programming burden as well
as the computation time.
Our study eventually leads to a comparison of the approximate MAM and the multi-
plicative mixed model, acquired from the full likelihood approach, by examining the
dierences in parameter estimates and statistical inference made from either approach,
using both real and simulated data. The comparison involves identifying theoretical
as well as practical issues associated with either model. One central area of focus is
the novel method used to estimate the condence intervals for the product contrasts,
introduced in [Brockho et al., 2015], and to investigate how these perform in com-
parison to the likelihood based condence intervals.
Outline of Chapters
Chapter 2 covers the theory of the mixed assessor model and exemplies its use on
real data. Chapter 3 presents a formal introduction to the multiplicative mixed model,
including a discussion on model identiability, the derivation of the likelihood function,
parameter estimation and prole likelihood based condence intervals.
Chapter 4
introduces the Laplace method for the approximation of the marginal likelihood for
the multiplicative mixed model, followed by a brief walk-through of the BFGS-method
used in the optimization of the Laplace likelihood. Lastly we apply the multiplicative
mixed model on real data, and compare the results from the multiplicative mixed
model and the MAM. Chapter 5 introduces the concepts of Automatic Dierentiation
(AD) and the Template Model Builder (TMB) for an alternative implementation of
the Laplace method that facilitates faster maximization of the Laplace likelihood.
We then compare the results of the TMB-based Laplace implementation with the
implementation in Chapter 4. In Chapter 6 and 7, we conduct our simulation studies
to assess the performance of the multiplicative mixed model and the MAM. Chapter 8
applies the classic two-way ANOVA model on real and simulated data to evaluate its
performance in comparison to the multiplicative mixed model and the MAM. Chapter
9 seeks to tie up and discuss the results and ndings and provide some suggestions for
future work. The conclusion is presented in Chapter 10.
==PAGE:15==
Chapter 2
The Mixed Assessor Model
The following chapter gives an in depth description of the Mixed Assessor Model
(MAM), explains the intuition behind the model, the justication of its use and covers
topics such as ANOVA decomposition, parameter estimation, statistical inference and
implementation.
The use of MAM is exemplied on real data; a data set that we
refer to as the food data. The presented data, originally provided by Dansk Sensorik
Center, has been analyzed in both [Brockho and Skovgaard, 1994] and [Brockho et
al., 2015] and stems from a sensory evaluation of ve food products.
Before setting our sights on the mixed assessor model introduced by [Brockho et al.,
2015], consider a more frequently used model for the analysis of sensory data - the
classic xed two-way ANOVA model.
Given a data set containing the scores of I
assessors for each of J products in K replicates, the xed two-way ANOVA model is
written as
yijk = µ + αi + νj + γij + ϵijk,
(2.1)
ϵijk ∼i.i.d. N(0, σ2),
where the response, yijk, denotes the score of product j given by assessor i in the k′th
replicate. The model contains the assessor main eect, αi, the product main eect,
νj, the panelist-by-product interaction eect, γij, and the random residual error, ϵijk.
It has been widely discussed whether to consider the assessor eect as random or as
xed, and although both types of analyses can be done with the proper interpretation
of results, having the assessor main eect as random is more compatible with the usual
purpose of doing such analyses; to draw inference about the tested products that may
generalize to a larger setting, and not merely to the particular assessors entering the
panel.
==PAGE:16==
4
The Mixed Assessor Model
Having the assessor main eect as random leads to the following mixed two-way
ANOVA model
yijk = µ + ai + νj + gij + ϵijk,
(2.2)
ai ∼i.i.d. N(0, σ2
a),
gij ∼i.i.d. N(0, σ2
g),
ϵijk ∼i.i.d. N(0, σ2),
where the Roman and Greek letters designate the random and xed eects, respec-
tively.
Although more properly specied than (2.1), the model may still not su-
ciently describe the complex variation in the data; the interaction term accounts not
only for the actual disagreement among assessors (personal preference), but also for
the variation resulting from the assessors' dierent use of scale. If, however, one could
distinguish between these two sources of variation, one would 1) gain more insight into
the panelists' performance, 2) obtain a more nuanced interpretation of the panelist-
by-product interaction, 3) increase the power to detect product dierences.
In [Brockho et al., 2015], the Mixed Assessor Model is introduced:
yijk = µ + ai + νj + βixj + dij + ϵijk,
(2.3)
ai ∼i.i.d. N(0, σ2
a), dij ∼i.i.d. N(0, σ2
d), ϵijk ∼i.i.d. N(0, σ),
where ai is the assessor main eect for i = 1, ..., I, νj is the product main eect for
j = 1, ..., J, dij is the random interaction term and ϵijk is the random residual error
for k = 1, ..., K with K being the number of replicates. Furthermore, xj = y.j. −y... is
the centered product average for product j, included as a covariate, and the regression
coecient, βi, is the individual scaling slope for assessor i, with PI
i=1 βi = 0. It is
assumed that the replications are included in the random sequence of assessments so
that the inclusion of block eects in the model is dispensable. Moreover, the MAM
approach is only applicable in situations with at least three products, providing a
sucient number of degrees of freedom to model both the scaling and the disagreement
eect. In situations with only two products or when three or more products basically
fall into two distinct levels, only the scaling eect can be modelled, since two points
can always be connected by a straight line.
The MAM is a simple linear mixed model since the xjs are derived directly from data
and are therefore considered known. The xjs act the role of a covariate to separate
the scaling eect from the panelist-by-product interaction. As a result, the interac-
tion term dij should capture the eect of real disagreement and not disagreement
attributable to dierent use of scale, which naturally leads to a more nuanced and
enhanced interpretation of the panelist-by-product interaction in comparison to (2.2).
Since the eect of disagreement is considered random, the xed part of the model,
µ + νj + βixj, is simply a regression line for each assessor versus the overall product
pattern.
There are a few things worth noting in regards to the MAM that are atypical. Firstly,
the assessor eect is considered random, and it would therefore seem reasonable to
consider the assessor dependent scaling eect as random as well, such that the indi-
vidual slopes, βi, are random and not xed. A random scaling eect would transform
==PAGE:17==
2.1 ANOVA-decomposition
5
(2.3) into a so-called random coecient model, but [Brockho et al., 2015] refers to a
previous study in [Næs et al., 2010] and argues that such a model is not appropriate
for this kind of analysis. Secondly, the model would have been more proper had the
covariate been the actual product eects, νj, instead of xj, which in turn would make
the model non-linear. But as stated in [Brockho et al., 2015], the MAM is only an
approximation to the following more properly specied non-linear mixed model
yijk = µ + ai + νj + biνj + dij + ϵijk,
(2.4)
ai ∼i.i.d. N(0, σ2
a), bi ∼i.i.d. N(0, σ2
b), dij ∼i.i.d. N(0, σ2
d), ϵijk ∼i.i.d. N(0, σ),
where the covariate has been substituted by the actual product eects, νj, and the
regression coecient, bi, is considered random. This is a so-called multiplicative mixed
model as a result of the multiplicative term biνj. This non-linear mixed model will be
discussed in detail in Chapter 3.

The mean squares are obtained by dividing the sum of squares by their respective
degrees of freedom, and these means squares are then used in F-tests to determine the
signicance of model eect. In the standard mixed two-way ANOVA, the denominator
in the F-statistic, testing the eect of product, is MSInteraction. In the MAM, however,
MSDisagreement is used instead.

and similarly for the multiplicative mixed model
Var(y.1. −y.2.) = 1
I σ2

(2.7)
The derivations of the two expressions above are given in Appendix A.
The latter expression has one additional term, which includes the scaling variance,
σb, and the actual product contrast; the greater the contrast, the greater the variance.
If the product contrast is small, however, the variance of the contrast for the multi-
plicative mixed model in (2.4) is close to the variance for the MAM. Moreover, under
the null hypothesis of no dierence between the two selected products, the additional
term vanishes, and the variance in the two models are equal. This proves a very import
point, as it implies that the test of the null hypothesis in the MAM is also valid under
the more properly specied model given in (2.4). This also means that the test for the
overall hypothesis of no product dierence
H0 : ν1 = ν2 = ... = νJ = 0,
is the same in both models.
This null hypothesis can be tested by setting up the
appropriate F-test for the signicance of the eect of products. Since the term con-
taining the scaling variance in 2.7 vanishes under the null hypothesis, an appropriate
F-statistic is
FP roduct =
MSP roduct
MSDisagreement ,
which follows an F-distribution with (J −1, (I −1)(J −2)) degrees of freedom under
the null hypothesis. Note that MSInteraction has been replaced by MSDisagreement
in the denominator, leading to an increased power to detect product dierences in
comparison to the standard mixed two-way ANOVA model.
Following a common hierarchical testing approach, the F-statistic to test the signi-
cance of the assessor main eect is
FAssessor =
MSAssessor
MSInteraction ,
==PAGE:20==
8
The Mixed Assessor Model
which follows an F-distribution with (I −1, (I −1)(J −1)) degrees of freedom under
the null hypothesis
H0 : σa = 0.
The F-statistic for the test of scaling dierences and thereby testing the presence of
non-linearity is
FScaling =
MSScaling
MSDisagreement ,
which follows an F-distribution with (I −1, (I −1)(J −1)) degrees of freedom under
the null hypothesis
H0 : β1 = β2 = ... = βI = 0.
Lastly, the F-statistic to test the presence of disagreement is
FDisagreement = MSDisagreement
MSError
,
which follows an F-distribution with ((I −1)(J −2), IJ(K −1)) degrees of freedom
under the null hypothesis of no disagreement eect
H0 : σd = 0.
From these F-statistics, together with the sums of squares, an ANOVA table for the
MAM can be constructed.

2.3
Condence Intervals
One key objective in sensory analyses is to draw inference about the products. In
particular, the interest lies in the ability to compare the various products and to
uncover signicant contrasts. For this reason, proper estimates of condence limits for
these contrasts are highly desirable.
In the standard two-way mixed model, the (1−α)100% condence interval is normally
found by
y.1. −y.2. ± t1−α/2(f)

IK

(2.9)
However, the MAM is an approximation to the multiplicative mixed model and we thus
want the condence limits to conform as much as possible to (2.4). The condence
band consists of all the values, δ0, that are not rejected by the hypothesis test H0 :
ν1 −ν2 = δ0. Under this null hypothesis, the variance of the contrast in model (2.4)
is given by

2.4
The Food Data
The following section provides a short introduction to data and presents also an ex-
ploratory analysis so as to summarize the characteristics of data as well as to exemplify
how scaling heterogeneity may manifest itself.
The presented data, originally provided by Dansk Sensorik Center, has been analyzed
in both [Brockho and Skovgaard, 1994] and [Brockho et al., 2015]. The data stems
from a sensory evaluation of ve food products (J = 5), where each product was
assessed by a panel of seven1 assessors (I = 7), scoring each product in four replicates
(K = 4) - yielding a total of 140 measurements. The products were assessed blindly,
and the replications are included in the random sequence of assessments, hence we
assume that no block eect is present. The assessment of a product leads to a product
score on a scale from 0-15.
Table 2.2 shows a small extract of the food data, which contains three factors, Assessor,
Product and Replicate, and y denotes the product score. For reasons of condentiality
the type of food is not revealed.

2.4.3
Scale Reversal
As mentioned in the introduction to data, the original data set contains the assess-
ments of eight assessors, but as [Brockho et al., 2015] argues, the product scores given
by assessor 8 should be removed from the data set due to scale reversal. The concept
of scale reversal is further explained in this section.
Figure 2.5 shows the scores given by assessor 8. The scores of this assessor are quite
atypical in comparison to the scores given by the other assessors shown in Figure 2.3.
As seen, assessor 8 prefers product 2 and 5 over product 1 and 4. This is even more
evident in Table 2.7, which compares the product averages for assessor 8 with the av-
erages for assessor 1-7 combined. The table clearly shows that assessor 8 has reversed
the scale completely.
0

15
y

Assessor 8
Figure 2.5: Product scores for assessor 8, who appears to have reversed the scale.
Table 2.7: Product averages for assessor 1-7 and for assessor 8.
Product
y(1−7)j.
y8j.

9.15
Consider the expectation of Yijk according to the MAM:
ˆE(Yijk) = ˆµ + ˆνj + ˆβixj
= ˆµ + y.j. + ˆβi(y.j. −y...)
= ˆµ + y.j. + ˆβi y.j. −ˆβi y...
= ˆµ + (1 + ˆβi) y.j. −ˆβi y...
From this it is seen that if ˆβi < −1, assessor i is expected to grade product j better
than product j′ if y.j. < y.j′.. In other words, if ˆβi < −1 assessor i in general gives
==PAGE:33==
2.4 The Food Data
21
lower scores the "better" the product is, which means that the assessor has reversed
the scale.
Table 2.8 shows the parameter estimates for the complete data set.
As expected
the scaling coecient for assessor 8 is below -1 (β8 = −2.11). By including the scores
from assessor 8, the estimated product eects change, with ν1 and ν4 estimated lower
than before and the rest of the eects estimated higher.
Hence, if an assessor has misunderstood the scale, and yet remain part of the sensory
analysis, the product eect estimates are dragged closer to each other, blurring the
real dierence between the products. Removing such assessors increases the chances
of detecting product contrasts.

==PAGE:35==
Chapter 3
The Multiplicative Mixed Model
The multiplicative mixed model belongs to the class of non-linear mixed eects models
(NLME), a class of models widely used for the analysis of repeated measures data in
dierent areas of statistics.
The NLME models are extensions of the linear mixed
models, allowing one or more xed and random eects to enter non-linearly in the
model function, such that the conditional expectation of the response given the random
eects becomes non-linear in its parameters.
This chapter gives a brief introduction to nonlinear mixed eects models, with an
emphasis on its general formulation and on the motivation for using this class of
models. This sets the stage for a formal introduction to the multiplicative mixed model,
including a discussion on model identiability, derivation of the likelihood function,
parameter estimation and prole likelihood based condence bands.
3.1
Non-Linear Mixed Eects Models
For one single level of grouping, the non-linear mixed eects model for the i'th group
and the j'th observation can be written in two stages, [Lindstrom and Bates, 1990].
The general two-stage representation is given as:
yij = f(φij, xij) + ϵij,
φij = Aijθ + Uijwi,
(3.1)
ϵij ∼i.i.d. N(0, σ2),
wi ∼N(0, Gi)
where f is a non-linear, real-valued, dierentiable function of a group specic param-
eter vector φij and a vector of covariates xij, and ϵij is the independently, normally
distributed within group error term. The matrices Aij and Uij of size r × p and r × q
are known design matrices for the xed and random eects, θ and wi, where wi is
normally distributed with mean 0 and variance-covariance matrix Gi. The subscript i
==PAGE:36==
24
The Multiplicative Mixed Model
allows the design matrices to incorporate varying xed and random eects for dierent
groups, and the subscript j allows the design matrices to contain covariates.
The entire response, yi, for the i'th group is then:

where U and G are block-diagonal matrices.
The two-stage representation of the
NLME can easily be extended to accommodate multilevel groupings and nested factors,
[Pinheiro and Bates, 2000].
The advantage of the two-stage representation is that
it simplies model specication for computer implementation, and it simplies the
calculation of derivatives for the model function with respect to θ and wi, [Pinheiro
and Bates, 2000].
One important question is why use non-linear mixed models? As a rst, mixed eects
models allow model parameters to vary among individuals, an intuitive and appealing
==PAGE:37==
3.1 Non-Linear Mixed Eects Models
25
feature. [Pinheiro and Bates, 2000] argues that non-linear models generally use fewer
parameters as oppose to approximate linear models, leading to more parsimonious
models, and that non-linear models may give more reliable predictions of the response
outside the observed range of data; they refer to polynomial ts as one type of linear
approximations that do not possess these properties. In addition, non-linear models
often include the theoretical considerations about the mechanisms producing the data,
which consequently grants the model parameters a natural and intuitive interpretation.
To exemplify this and to illustrate the use of the two-stage representation, consider
the logistic growth curve model, employed in [Pinheiro and Bates, 2000] to analyze
the growth pattern of two types of soybean plants
yij = f(φi, tij) + ϵij =

where yij is the leaf weight of soybean plant i at time j. The three parameters θ1, θ2
and θ3 represent the xed population parameters, where θ1 determines the asymptotic
leaf weight, θ2 is the time at which a leaf reaches half its asymptotic weight, and θ3 is
related to the growth rate. The random eects, wi, represents each plants individual
deviation from the xed population parameters. [Pinheiro and Bates, 2000] also uses
the growth curve model to demonstrate its superiority to a polynomial t that both
lacks the physical interpretation of the model parameters and requires twice as many
parameters to give a comparable t.
These benets of the NLME usually come at the cost of higher computational expenses.
The reason is that the likelihood function for the NLME model does not admit a
closed-form solution when the random eects enter the model in a non-linear fashion,
making data non-normal. This consequently leads to more computationally demanding
numerical methods to estimate the model parameters - especially when the number of
random eects is large.

The Multiplicative Mixed Model
3.2
The Multiplicative Mixed Model
In [Brockho et al., 2015], the Mixed Assessor Model (MAM) is introduced for the
analysis of sensory and consumer data. The MAM stands as a linear approximation
to what [Brockho et al., 2015] refers to as a more properly specied multiplicative
mixed model.
The multiplicative mixed model is given as:
yijk = µ + ai + νj + biνj + dij + ϵijk,
(3.3)
ai ∼i.i.d. N(0, σ2
a),
bi ∼i.i.d. N(0, σ2
b),
dij ∼i.i.d. N(0, σ2
d),
ϵijk ∼i.i.d. N(0, σ2),
(3.4)
where the Roman and the Greek letters designate the random and xed eects, re-
spectively. The model contains the main eect of assessor ai, a product eect νj, an
assessor specic scaling eect bi, a panelist-by-product disagreement eect dij, and
the score yijk. The model follows a two-way ANOVA setup containing also a non-
linear regression term biνj, a regression for each assessor against the centered product
eects.
It is assumed that observations across assessors are independent, that the
random eects are independent, and that ϵijk is independent of the random eects.
Although all NLME models may be formulated according to (3.1), [Lindstrom and
Bates, 1990]), it is somewhat unclear how to apply the general two-stage representation
to the multiplicative mixed model. The xed eect parameters and the random eect
coecients do not enter in pairs in the multiplicative mixed model as they do in (3.2).
Our suggestion on how to write the model using the two-stage representation is
yijk = f(φij) + ϵijk = φ1,ij + φ2,ij + φ3,ij · φ2,ij + ϵijk,
φij = Aijθ + Uijwi,
where i = 1, . . . , I, j = 1, . . . , J and k = 1, . . . , K denote the assessor level, prod-
uct level and replicate, respectively.
The vector θ contains the xed eects, θ =
[µ, ν1, ..., νJ]T , and the vector wi contains all the random eect coecients belong-
ing to assessor i, i.e. wi = [ai, bi, di1, . . . , diJ]T . To exemplify, let J = 3 and K = 2,
then for j = 1

simple representation will be used.

(3.5)

3.2.1
Model Identiability
The xed part of the model in (3.3) is written µ+νj, which leaves J +1 parameters to
be estimated. Only J degrees of freedom are, however, required to describe the eect
of products. For this reason, we induce restrictions on our parameters to reduce the
degrees of freedom and thus to avoid identiability issues when estimating the model.
In our implementation, µ is set to zero to overcome the identiability issue, leading to
the following model expression
yijk = ai + νj + bi(νj −¯ν) + dij + ϵijk,
(3.8)
ai ∼i.i.d. N(0, σ2
a),
bi ∼i.i.d. N(0, σ2
b),
dij ∼i.i.d. N(0, σ2
d),
ϵijk ∼i.i.d. N(0, σ2).
In a parametric model, the identiability of the model ensures that no two sets of
model parameters give rise to the same distribution of data, making it possible, in
theory, to learn the true values of the model parameters with an innite amount of
data. This property is a neseccsity in order to ensure consistent inference. A necessary
condition to certify the identiability of a model, is to prove the uniqueness of the rst
two moments as a function of the model parameters, i.e. to show that
E(Y|θ = θ[1]) = E(Y|θ = θ[2]),
V(θ[1], σ[1], Ψ[1]) = V(θ[2], σ[2], Ψ[2]),
imply θ[1] = θ[2], σ[1] = σ[2] and Ψ[1] = Ψ[2], where Ψ = [σa, σb, σd]T . For models
with normally distributed data this condition is not only necessary, but also sucient,
since the normal distribution is uniquely determined by the rst two moments, [Eugene,
2004]. The uniqueness of the rst moment of the multiplicative mixed model is easily
recognized, since
E(Yijk|ν[1]j) = E(Yijk|ν[2]j)
with
E(Yijk|ν[1]j) = ν[1]j,
E(Yijk|ν[2]j) = ν[2]j,
is satised only if ν[1]j = ν[2]j for all j.
==PAGE:42==
30
The Multiplicative Mixed Model
The re-formulation of the mixed multiplicative model in (3.8) naturally alters the for-
mulation of the covariance structure

Recall that the covariance matrix, V(θ, σ, Ψ), is a block diagonal matrix with identical
blocks, for what reason it is sucient to prove the uniqueness of the parameters in one
such block
V1(θ, σ[1], Ψ[1]) = V1(θ, σ[2], Ψ[2]).
By pairing the matrices, we obtain a set of equations, which are then solved with
respect to the variance components. To ensure uniqueness, the number of distinct
products must be at least two. So for J = 2, we obtain the following ve equations.

Solving these with respect to σ1, σ2, Ψ1 and Ψ2 gives the solution
σ1 = σ2,
Ψ1 = Ψ2.
The equations have been solved using Maple 18 - see Appendix C. The result proves
that the multiplicative mixed model is identiable.
==PAGE:43==
3.2 The Multiplicative Mixed Model
31
3.2.2
The Likelihood Function
The xed eects and the variance components in (3.8) are found by the maximum
likelihood method. In other words
(ˆθ, ˆσ, ˆΨ) = argmax
(θ,σ,Ψ)
L(θ, σ, Ψ; y),
where L(θ, σ, Ψ; y) is the marginal likelihood. The marginal likelihood is the probabil-
ity density of y given the parameters (θ, σ, Ψ), but where the density is a function of
the parameters, and y is considered xed, [Pinheiro and Bates, 2000]. With p denoting
the probability density, then
L(θ, σ, Ψ; y) = p(y|θ, σ, Ψ).
The marginal probability density, p(y|θ, σ, Ψ), is found by integrating over the random
eects, w, in the joint probability density function, p(y, w|θ, σ, Ψ), [Christensen and
Brockho, 2012]. The likelihood function is thus
L(θ, σ, Ψ; y) = p(y|θ, σ, Ψ)
=
Z
Rq p(y, w|θ, σ, Ψ)dw
=
Z
Rq p(y|θ, σ, w)p(w|Ψ)dw,
where q is the number of random eects. The integrand is also called the joint likeli-
hood, i.e LJ(θ, σ, Ψ; y, w) = p(y, w|θ, σ, Ψ).
Unfortunately this q-dimensional integral does not have a closed-form solution in gen-
eral and can therefore be very dicult to solve. When data is normally distributed,
however, as it is in our case, the above integral does in fact have a closed-form solu-
tion. Assuming normality for the residual error and the random eects, the conditional
distribution of Y given the random eects is
W ∼N(0, Ψ)
Y|W = w ∼N(f(θ, w), σ2I),
where f(θ, w) is our model function. If the random eects enter the model linearly,
as they do in the multiplicative model, it follows that the marginal distribution of Y
is a normal distribution with mean and covariance
E(Y) = fx(θ)
Cov(Y) = V,
where fx(θ) is the xed part of the model, and V is the covariance matrix for the
data, [Madsen and Thyregod, 2010].
Since the marginal likelihood is equal to the
==PAGE:44==
32
The Multiplicative Mixed Model
marginal distribution of Y, the closed-form expression for the likelihood is
L(θ, σ, Ψ; y) = p(y|θ, σ, Ψ)
= (2π)−n

3.2 The Multiplicative Mixed Model
33
3.2.3
Prole Likelihood Based Condence Intervals
The prole likelihood for a model parameter η is dened as
Lp(η; y) = max
η∗L(η, η∗; y)
where η∗= {η \ η} and η is a vector consisting of the xed eects and the variance
components, η = [θ, σ, Ψ]T . In other words, for every point in the prole likelihood
function, the parameter of interest, η, is held constant while the likelihood is optimized
with respect to the remaining parameters, η∗, [Christensen and Brockho, 2012].
The prole likelihood can be used to make likelihood based condence intervals for
the parameters using Wilk's likelihood ratio test, which states that the test statistic
χ2 = −2 log

Lp(η0; y)
max(Lp(η; y))

= −2 log
Lp(η0; y)
L(ˆη; y)

is approximately χ2-distributed with 1 degree of freedom under the H0 hypothesis
H0 : η = η0.
According to [Arizona, 2009], the log-likelihood ratio test statistic converges to a χ2
1-
distribution under the H0 hypothesis whenever the maximum likelihood estimator is
asymptotically normally distributed around the true parameter values.
The 100(1 −α)%-condence interval for the parameter of interest, η, thus consists of
all the values that satises
Lp(η; y)
L(ˆη; y) > e−1/2χ2
1−α(1),
where the quantity on the left side of the inequality is called the relative prole like-
lihood, [Madsen and Thyregod, 2010]. The likelihood based condence intervals have
the very important feature that they can be asymmetric. This is not the case when
the condence intervals are estimated by the standard method based on the Student's
t-distribution together with variance estimates.
Furthermore, the troublesome task of deriving appropriate variance estimates is need-
less when employing the likelihood based method. On the other hand, it can be quite
computationally expensive to compute the number of points needed to obtain a su-
ciently smooth relative prole likelihood curve, since the likelihood has to be optimized
for each point on the curve. As an alternative, the prole likelihood can be interpolated
by a spline to obtain a smooth curve with a relatively small number of optimizations.
==PAGE:46==
34
The Multiplicative Mixed Model
==PAGE:47==
Chapter 4
The Laplace Method
As stated previously, the marginal likelihood of data, arising from models in which
random eects enter non-linearly, does in general not admit a closed-form solution. As
a consequence, the evaluation of the log-likelihood function of the response requires the
troublesome evaluation of a multiple integral, which is why an approximation of the
integral is desirable. One such approximation, often used within the framework of non-
linear mixed eects models, is the Laplace method, [Vonesh, 1996], [Wolnger and Lin,
1997], [Pinheiro and Bates, 1995]. Due to a potential increase in computation speed,
the Laplace method may still be advantageous in situations where data is normally
distributed, and the log-likelihood function therefore has a closed-form expression -
we elaborate on this in Section 5.
The following chapter introduces the Laplace method and demonstrates how to esti-
mate the parameters of the multiplicative mixed model using the Laplace likelihood.
The approximation and optimization of the Laplace likelihood is implemented in R,
and the implementation follows the approach shown in [Christensen and Brockho,
2012]. A short walk-through of the BFGS-method is presented for the optimization
of the Laplace likelihood. Lastly, the use of the Laplace method is exemplied on
the food data introduced in Section 2.4, and we compare the parameter estimates and
statistical inference of this likelihood approach with the results obtained by the MAM.
The Laplace method approximates integrals on the form
Z b
a
eh(x)dx,
where h(x) is a twice dierentiable function.

36
The Laplace Method
Consider the marginal likelihood,

h(θ, σ, Ψ, w; y) = log (p(y|θ, σ, w)p(w|Ψ))
= log (p(y|θ, σ, w)) + log (p(w|Ψ)).
(4.2)
The Laplace method employs a second order Taylor series expansion around the max-

(4.7)
The Laplace approximation has the nice property of not placing any restrictions on how
the random eects enter the model function and thus supports both nested, crossed,
partially crossed random eects etc. [Christensen and Brockho, 2012].
Note that whenever w and ϵ are normally distributed, and w appears linearly in the
model function, i.e. data is normally distributed, the second order Taylor expansion is
exact. This can be seen from (4.2), since log (p(y|θ, σ, w)) and log (p(w|Ψ)) become
polynomials of second degree with respect to w when the aforementioned conditions
are fullled, such that their third derivatives are all zero, making the second order
Taylor expansion exact. In other words, the Laplace approximation is exact when-
ever data is normally distributed. In the case of non-normal data, the exactness of
the Taylor expansion depends on the amount of data. When the number of observa-
tions per random eect increases, the joint log-likelihood in (4.2) tends to a quadratic
function, meaning that the exactness of the second order Taylor expansion increases,
[Christensen and Brockho, 2012]. Seen from another perspective, we may view the
Laplace method as a method which approximates the integrand in 4.1, i.e the joint
likelihood, by a Gaussian curve. When the joint log-likelihood tends to a quadratic
function, the integrand (the joint likelihood) gets closer to a Gaussian curve, and the
Laplace likelihood gets closer to the true marginal likelihood function. The fact that
the joint probability density function approaches a Gaussian curve when the number
of observations approaches innity is supported by the Bayesian central limit theorem,
[Christensen and Brockho, 2012].
The approximate log-likelihood in (4.7) contains the Hessian, which can be expensive
to compute. However, the second order term in the Hessian,
H(θ, σ, Ψ, ˜w) = f ′T
w V−1f ′
w −f ′′T
wwV−1(y −f) + G−1,
usually contributes negligible compared to the remaining terms, [Pinheiro and Bates,
2000], and is thus discarded, yielding
˜H(θ, σ, Ψ, ˜w) = f ′T
w V−1f ′
w + G−1.
(4.8)
==PAGE:50==
38
The Laplace Method
The approximate Hessian has the advantage of requiring only rst-order partial deriva-
tives of the model function with respect to the random eect coecients. The Laplace
method using ˜H instead of H has come to known as the modied Laplace method,
[Pinheiro and Bates, 1995]. It is worth noting that the second order term ignored in
˜H equals zero when the random eects enter the model linearly, such that ˜H = H,
making the modied Laplace method exact as well. Via automatic dierentiation, it
is possible to operate with the exact Hessian without compromising the computation
speed - we return to this in Section 5
4.1
Model Estimation
[Christensen and Brockho, 2012] suggests an iterative procedure consisting of iterat-
ing back and forth between estimating ˜w for xed ˆθ, ˆσ and ˆΨ and vice versa, which
then leads to a nested optimization problem; every evaluation of the Laplace likeli-
hood in the outer optimization requires the vector of random eects coecients, ˜w,
and the approximate Hessian, ˜H(θ, σ, Ψ, ˜w), obtained from the inner optimization.
These quantities are obtained by rstly optimizing the joint log-likelihood function, h,
w.r.t. w for xed ˆθ, ˆσ and ˆΨ, and then secondly deriving the Jacobian, f ′
w, by a nite
dierence approximation to construct ˜H(θ, σ, Ψ, ˜w).
Note that the variance parameters are evaluated on a logarithmic scale allowing these
to become arbitrarily close to zero, thus making the estimation unbounded. This makes
the log-likelihood surface more quadratic to facilitate faster convergence and allows the
optimization to proceed smoothly even when the maximum likelihood estimate is zero,
[Christensen and Brockho, 2012].
4.1.1

The uncertainty of both the xed eect estimates and the variance component esti-

4.2
Quasi-Newton Method - BFGS
Since the true Hessian, ∇2lLA, is cumbersome, not to say expensive to derive or
estimate, a quasi-Newton method stands as an attractive optimizer, as it does not
require computations of the Hessian. In place of the true Hessian, the method makes
use of an approximation, Bk, which is updated at each iteration to account for the
additional information about the second derivative of lLA along the search direction,
provided by changes in the gradient.
For the purpose of illustration, let the objective function, f, be a twice continuously
dierentiable function. At the current iterate, xk, the Newton direction is derived by
rstly approximating f by a second order Taylor series expansion around xk
mk(p) = fk + ∇f T
k p + 1
2pT ∇2fkp ≈f(xk + p).
(4.11)
The classic Newton direction is the vector, p, minimizing (4.11), which is obtained by
setting ∇mk(p) equal to zero and solving for p
∇mk(p) = ∇fk + ∇2fkp = 0,
pk = −(∇2fk)−1∇fk.
Instead of computing the Hessian afresh at each iteration, a quasi-Newton method
approximates the Hessian,
Bk ≈∇2fk,
which is then updated after each iteration to account for the curvature measured during
the latest step. To achieve this, consider a new quadratic model at a new iterate xk+1
mk+1(p) = fk+1 + ∇f T
k+1p + 1
2pT Bk+1p,
xk+1 = xk + αkpk,
where is αk is the step length, chosen to satisfy the Wolfe conditions, [Nocedal and
Wright, 2006]. For Bk to have the desirable properties of the true Hessian, ∇mk+1(p)
must also match the gradient of f at xk, i.e.
∇mk+1(−αkpk) = ∇fk+1 −αkBk+1pk = ∇fk.
By rearranging, we obtain
Bk+1αkpk = ∇fk+1 −∇fk.
(4.12)
==PAGE:52==
40
The Laplace Method
For convenience, dene the two vectors
sk = αkpk = xk+1 −xk,
yk = ∇fk+1 −∇fk,
then (4.12) can be written as
Bk+1sk = yk,
which is known as the secant equation. So given a change in the gradient from one
iterate to the next, the positive denite matrix Bk+1 maps the change in sk into yk.
This is only possible when sk and yk satisfy the curvature condition, [Nocedal and
Wright, 2006].
sT
k yk > 0.
When the curvature condition is met, which it is when the step length satisfy the Wolfe
Conditions, [Nocedal and Wright, 2006], there is a solution, Bk, to the secant equation
- in fact there exists an innite number of solution. To ensure the uniqueness of Bk+1,
an additional constraint is imposed requiring that Bk+1, among all possible positive
symmetric matrices, is the matrix closest to the current Bk. This involves solving the
problem
min
B ||B −Bk||,
subject to B = BT ,
Bsk = yk
(4.13)
The most popular quasi-Newton algorithm is the BFGS method, and the updating pro-
cedure for the Hessian of this method, satisfying the secant equation, whilst ensuring
the uniqueness of Bk i.e. satisfying (4.13), is given by
Bk+1 = Bk −BksksT
k Bk
sT
k Bksk
+ ykyT
k
yT
k sk
.
The minimization problem does not explicitly require the updated Hessian to be posi-
tive denite; the updating procedure preserves the positive deniteness of Bk+1 when-
ever Bk is positive denite. An often used initial B0 is the identity matrix. Despite
being a potentially bad estimate, the BFGS update has proven to possess eective
self-correcting properties, and tends to correct itself within a few steps, [Nocedal and
Wright, 2006].
The BFGS method is employed via the optimizer, nlminb, from the base package in
R, and it is used for both the inner and outer optimizations in our implementation of
the Laplace method. Since the rst derivative of the Laplace likelihood, lLA, is not
available either, nlminb obtains it by a nite dierence approximation.

M1 : yijk = ai + νj + bi(νj −¯ν) + dij + ϵijk,
M2 : yijk = ai + νj + bi(νj −¯ν) + ϵijk,
M3 : yijk = ai + νj + dij + ϵijk,
M4 : yijk = νj + bi(νj −¯ν) + dij + ϵijk,
M5 : yijk = νj + ϵijk,
M6 : yijk = µ + ϵijk.
(4.14)

4.1, alongside the condence intervals of the mixed assessor model (blue). As seen, the
condence intervals of P2-P3, P2-P5, P3-P5 and P1-P4 enclose zero, and the contrasts
are thus insignicant at α = 0.05.
Although less obvious than in Section 2.4.2, the condence intervals are asymmetric
and become more asymmetric as the absolute size of the contrast grows. In addition,
the width of the condence bands for the contrasts in Table 4.5 are noticeably smaller
than the width of the condence bands seen in Table 2.6. Peculiarly, the p-values of
the contrasts are just slightly larger now compared to the p-values found in the MAM
analysis. This means that the contrasts found by the multiplicative mixed model are
less signicant, despite the more narrow condence intervals. For the large contrasts,
the wider left bands of the condence intervals for the multiplicative mixed model
appear to be the explanation for this. This does not hold for the small contrasts, how-
ever, as the left bands are now shorter, and yet the p-values attain higher values. So
for small contrasts, the higher p-values are instead attributable to smaller contrast es-
timates, which counteract the narrow condence intervals to produce higher p-values.
Put dierently, the slightly higher contrast estimates for the small contrasts obtained
by the MAM, increase the signicance of the contrasts, despite their wider condence
intervals.

==PAGE:61==
Chapter 5
Template Model Builder
The Laplace likelihood, implemented as described in Section 4.3, has shown to be very
computationally exhaustive to optimize, making it nearly impossible to conduct the
simulation studies of Section 6 and 7.3. [Christensen and Brockho, 2012] suggests a
few ways to increase computation speed such as 1) supplying the true gradient of the
joint log-likelihood with respect to the random eects, which increases the rate of con-
vergence of the inner optimization, 2) supplying the analytical Jacobian of the model
function with respect to the random eects so as to increase the speed of approxi-
mating the Hessian of the joint log-likelihood function, 3) proling out the residual
variance component, σ, of the Laplace likelihood function. It is our believe, however,
that these speed improvements would not be sucient to allow us to conduct our sim-
ulation studies. As such it was found necessary to seek an alternative implementation
of the Laplace likelihood for what reason we considered automatic dierentiation and
the TMB-package.
All R-code related to this chapter is shown in Appendix E.7, E.8, E.9 and E.10.
The Template Model Builder (TMB) is a recently developed R-package, [Kristensen,
2014], that enables fast maximization of the Laplace likelihood approximation to the
marginal log-likelihood.
The maximization is performed by a standard non-linear
optimization algorithm (e.g.
BFGS), which takes as input the Laplace likelihood
and its rst derivative.
These are obtained by Automatic Dierentiation (AD) of
the joint log-likelihood of data and the random eect coecients.
The user needs
to dene the negative joint log-likelihood as a C++ template function, while all other
remaining operations are performed in R. The following sections introduce the concept
of automatic dierentiation with an emphasis on the benets of AD as well as TMB.
We present a short description of the implementation of the Laplace method via TMB,
which is subsequently used for the analysis of the food data, followed by a comparison
with the previous Laplace implementation in terms of parameter estimates, inference
and computation time.
==PAGE:62==
50
Template Model Builder
5.1
Automatic Dierentiation
Automatic dierentiation is chain rule based technique that is used to compute accu-
rate derivatives of a function, and in doing so, utilizes the fact that all calculations
done by a computer can be broken down into a long sequence of simple operations
(+, −, ·, /, log, sin, √, . . . ). The computer evaluates the function and keeps track of
all the operations used and then builds a representation of the function, also known
as a tape or a computational graph, [Kristensen et al.]. There exists two main types
of automatic dierentiation referred to as forward mode and reverse mode. In reverse
mode the program runs the tape backwards and uses the chain rule to update the
derivatives one operation at a time, beginning with the model function at hand, [Mad-
sen and Thyregod, 2010]. The derivatives are calculated in a similar fashion in the
forward mode, beginning with the independent variables.
For illustration, consider the following function
f(x1, x2) = (x1 + x2)2 + sin(x2),
(5.1)
with the partial derivatives
∂f
∂x1 = 2(x1 + x2),
∂f
∂x2 = 2(x1 + x2) cos(x2).
(5.2)
Figure 5.1 shows the computational graph for f(x1, x2), and Table 5.1 gives an overview
of the intermediate variables stored in the tape. As seen, four simple operations are
required to evaluate the function.

+

mode, respectively. It is seen that they both, as expected, end up with partial deriva-
tives shown in (5.2). An overview of the intermediate derivatives is given in Table 5.2
and 5.3. In forward mode, the computation of derivatives needs to be seeded in the
beginning in order to distinguish between the derivative with respect to x1 and the
derivative with respect to x2. As an example, if w′

only one single run is required in reverse mode if f(x1, x2) is a scalar function.

Template Model Builder
Further, it can be proven that the following inequality holds under forward mode,
[Griewank and Walther, 2008]
C
 ∂f
∂xi

< 4C(f),
i = 1, ..., xn,
⇒
C(∇f) < 4nC(f),
and similarly under reverse mode
C(∇f) < 4C(f),
where C(f) is the cost of evaluating f, measured as the number of simple operations
needed in the evaluation. This means that reverse mode is, most often, superior to
forward mode. Furthermore, it should be noted that the cost of nding the gradient
by reverse mode AD is only dependent of the cost of evaluating f and not by the
number of variables. These results are rather surprising, since the cost of nding the
derivatives by the nite dierence method is (n + 1)C(f) operations, [Fournier et al.,
2012]. The nite dierence method is thus about as costly as forward mode AD, but
it is considerably less accurate than automatic dierentiation methods, which empha-
sizes the benet of AD, [Griewank and others, 1989].
There is however one practical concern with AD - the memory of the computer. All
the intermediate variables and derivatives need to be stored, and if the computational
graph contains many simple operations, the iterative procedure becomes lengthy, and
the memory requirements become immense, [Madsen and Thyregod, 2010]. The tapes
may, however, be optimized by smart implementation as shown in [Kristensen et al.],
where the the advantages of the CppAD-library for automatic dierentiation in C++
is described.
First order derivatives are usually sucient for the maximization of likelihood func-
tions. If however, the likelihood function is complex, up to third order derivatives can
be necessary. These are calculated in a similar manner by using the already taped
calculations of the rst order derivatives. Forward or reverse mode AD is applied on
this tape to create a new tape of the operations needed to calculate the second order
derivatives, and this tape can then be used to nd the third order derivatives.
5.2
The Principle Behind TMB
The Laplace approximation to the marginal log-likelihood cannot be described by a
straightforward sequence of simple operations due to the inner optimization in the
approximation. Regardless, TMB facilitates the maximization of the Laplace approx-
imation by using up to third order derivatives obtained by clever application of au-
tomatic dierentiation. AD is used to compute the gradient and the Hessian of the
joint log-likelihood with respect to the random eect coecients. These quantities
are then used by Newtons method in the inner optimization, and the Hessian is also
used in the Laplace approximation itself. AD is used to nd the derivatives of order
three of the joint log-likelihood, which is used to compute the gradient of the Laplace
==PAGE:65==
5.3 The Advantages of TMB
53
likelihood. This gradient and the Laplace approximation itself is given as input to
the R-function nlminb, which then optimizes the Laplace likelihood and returns the
maximum likelihood parameters.
5.2.1
Uncertainty of Parameter Estimates
The uncertainty of the parameter estimates can be obtained by the TMB, which esti-
mates the joint covariance of the random coecients, the xed eect parameters and
the variance components by
Cov
 
ˆw
ˆη
!
≈
 
−(∇2
wℓJ( ˆw))−1
0
0
0
!
+ J Var(ˆη) JT ,
(5.3)
where η = [θ, σ, Ψ]T , J is the Jacobian of [ ˆw(η), η]T with respect to η, and Var(ˆη)
is calculated by (4.9) in Section 4.1.1. The rst term represents the conditional vari-
ance given both the xed eects and the variance components, and the second term
accounts for the variance of the conditional mean of [ ˆw, ˆη]T with respect to the xed
eects, [Kristensen, 2013]. Due to the second term, the variance for the random eect
coecients is estimated more accurately compared to (4.10). The variance estimate
in (4.10) does not use this second term due to the diculties of computing J. By so-
phisticated use of AD, the Template Model Builder is able to derive J to estimate the
covariance matrix in (5.3). The diagonal in this matrix then consists of the variance
of all the model parameters, estimated in a proper manner.
5.3
The Advantages of TMB
The Template Model Builder package is very useful for complex non-linear mixed
models containing numerous of parameters, and it is designed to be fast for models
containing up to 106 random eects and 103 xed eects. Its purpose is similar to the
established AD Model Builder, but TMB is favorable in regard to computation time.
As shown in [Kristensen et al.], speedups ranging from 1.5 to about 100, with greatest
gain for large problems, can be achieved by TMB. Such gains are achievable since
TMB utilizes several existing high-performance libraries compared to the AD Model
Builder, which is coded basically from scratch. These high-performance libraries in-
clude CppAD for automatic dierentiation in C++, which can perform forward and
reverse mode of any order. The computation cost is minimized by employing an opti-
mal combination of these modes.
The R-library Matrix and the C++-library Eigen are used for sparse and dense ma-
trix calculations. If the Hessian of the joint log-likelihood with respect to the random
eects, H in (4.7), is sparse, TMB can operate on very high dimensional problems,
[Kristensen et al.]. Figure 5.4 shows the sparsity pattern of the Hessian for the food
data from Section 2.4, conrming that it is a sparse matrix. Only random eect coef-
cients belonging to the same assessor result in non-zero elements.
==PAGE:66==
54
Template Model Builder

Figure 5.4: The food data: Sparsity pattern of the Hessian of the joint log-likelihood with
respect to the random eects.
The marked elements in the matrix indicate
non-zero elements.
Given the random eect coecients, the observations become independent, and this
conditional independence structure yields a sparse Hessian of the joint log-likelihood
with respect to data and the random eect coecients.
This Hessian is also the
precision matrix for the joint distribution of data and the random eect coecients.
According to [Kristensen et al.] it is possible to perform the Laplace approximation for
models with a very large number of random eects, if this precision matrix is sparse.
Figure 5.5a shows the sparsity pattern, conrming that the precision matrix is indeed
sparse. The lower right corner of the this precision matrix is equal to the Hessian
in Figure 5.4. The sparsity of this part of the matrix is of great importance, since
the Hessian, as mentioned, is part of the Laplace approximation itself. In addition,
the Hessian is used in Newtons method for the inner optimization. TMB is better
equipped than ADMB to take maximal advantage of the sparsity structures, by de-
termining these structures automatically. This contributes to the before mentioned
speedups.
The sparsity pattern of the inverse precision matrix is shown in Figure 5.5b.
The
upper left block diagonal shows the sparsity structure of the covariance matrix for
the observations in (3.7). Since the covariance is a block-diagonal matrix, the inverse
covariance matrix has the same structure. The inverse covariance matrix is part of the
closed-form solution in (3.10), and as such the density of this matrix is of great impor-
tance, if the closed-form expression is maximized directly. For the multiplicative mixed
model, this block structure can be exploited (as in 3.10) to reduce the computational
cost. However, for models assuming dependency between observations from dierent
assessors, the inverse covariance matrix is in risk of becoming dense and troublesome to
handle due to its size. As an example, one could assume dependency between observa-
tions coming from the same replicate if the experiment is not randomized suciently,
thus creating a possible block eect. In this case, the inverse covariance matrix would
==PAGE:67==
5.3 The Advantages of TMB
55
be dense, and a direct maximization of the closed-form expression of the likelihood
suddenly becomes computationally expensive. This can be avoided by maximizing the
Laplace approximation of the log-likelihood by the TMB, such that the sparsity of the
precision matrix is exploited.

the data section, the user species the response vector, y, and the factors Assessor,
Product and AssessorProduct. All model parameters are specied in the parameter
section as shown. The additional four lines of code (line 22-25) add the non-log ver-
sions of the variance components into the output report. Lastly the user denes the
negative joint log-likelihood function.

The rst line of code, in the R-script shown below, compiles the C++ template func-
tion. The second line is left as is, but make sure that the string matches the name of
the C++ template. The function MakeADFun takes as input the data frame as well as a
list of parameters specifying the initial values of the parameters. The vector, random,
determines which parameters to be considered as random - parameters not included
in this vector are by default considered xed.
The function MakeADFun returns an object containing the Laplace likelihood and its
gradient, which are supplied to nlminb for the optimization. After the optimization
of the Laplace likelihood, sdreport computes, among other things, the Hessian of the
Laplace likelihood, the uncertainty of both the xed eect parameters and the vari-
ance components, the random eect coecients, the uncertainty of the random eect
coecients, the non-log versions of the variance components and the corresponding
covariance matrix and the joint precision matrix of the joint log-likelihood function.

For the sake of convenience, the implementation of the Laplace method via TMB is
referred to as L2, and correspondingly, let the early implementation be referred to
as L1. The soul purpose of re-analyzing the food data with L2 is to demonstrate its
superiority over L1 in terms of speed.
The tables below show the strong similarities in parameter estimates between L1 and
L2. Do note that the uncertainty of ai and bi are no longer underestimated, as L2
takes into account the uncertainty of the xed eect parameters, a feature not easily
obtained with L1.

5.5 The Food Data Revisited
59
The speed of L2 is simply astonishing and went far beyond our expectations. With
the function call fitLA(data, stats = FALSE), L1 returns an object containing the
xed eect parameters and the negative log-likelihood in roughly 891.21 seconds, or
equivalently, 14.85 minutes. L2, on the other hand, completes the optimization in 0.46
seconds (average time across 100 optimizations) and returns not only the aforemen-
tioned quantities, but supplies also (among other things) the Hessian of the Laplace
likelihood, the uncertainty of both the xed eect parameters and the variance compo-
nents, the random eect coecients, the uncertainty of the random eect coecients,
the non-log versions of the variance components and the corresponding covariance
matrix and the joint precision matrix of the joint log-likelihood function.
If stats = TRUE, L1 returns the above quantities, but as a consequence, the compu-
tation time increases to 116.30 minutes, making L2 roughly 15169 times faster than
L1 on this particular data set. Figure 5.6 shows the relative prole likelihoods for the
10 product contrasts. Each prole likelihood requires on average 28 optimizations,
summarizing to 283 optimizations in total to construct all 10 proles. L2 performed
the task in 35 seconds. In comparison L1 performed this task in roughly 4 days, de-
spite using as low as 15 optimizations per prole. The speed gain of TMB allows us
to evaluate the prole likelihood on a ner grid and thus to obtain a more accurate
prole in much less time.
As a result, L2 is used for the remainder of our study, which among other things
include simulation studies of 2000 data sets.

==PAGE:73==
Chapter 6
Simulation Study
Simulation studies provide excellent means of uncovering strengths and limitations of
models to aid in the overall assessment of model performance. The objective of the
simulation study presented in this chapter is thus to evaluate the performance of the
multiplicative mixed model and the mixed assessor model. To accomplish this, 2000
data sets are generated using the exact same experimental setup as in the food data, so
that each data set consists of 4 repeated sensory evaluations of 5 products from each of
7 assessors. The data sets are simulated according to the multiplicative mixed model,
and to retain a reasonable degree of realism, the true model parameters are not chosen
at random, but rather set to the parameter estimates shown in Table 4.3.
Recall
that these values are the estimators of our early Laplace likelihood approximation.
The random eect coecients of each random eect are drawn independently from
identical normal distributions with mean 0 and variance ˆσa, ˆσb and ˆσd, respectively.
All R-code related to this chapter is found in Appendix B, E.10, E.11, E.12, E.13 and
E.14. Note that the R-package SensMixed, [Kuznetsova et al., 2014b], is used in the
simulation study to estimate the condence intervals of the product contrasts for the
MAM. The R-package oers higher computation speed, in this regard, compared to
our own implementation.
As a rst, the MAM is applied to each data set to derive the average parameter
estimates presented in Table 6.1. As seen, the product estimates as well as ˆσ appear
to be unbiased, but surprisingly and for reasons not entirely clear, the average estimate
of σa reveals a downward bias. One plausible explanation is that the "low" number of
replicates does not allow for suciently accurate estimates of ai, which in conjunction
with (only) 7 assessors leads to uncertain estimates of σa. If so, the observed bias
arise merely as a result of uncertainty rather than true bias. Quite oppositely, the
average estimate of σd is 0.34, despite the true value being nearly zero, and diculties
in accurately estimating variance components thus appear to arise, when the true
parameter value lies near the boundary of its parameter space.

Simulation Study

estimator is well known to be downward biased, although less pronounced for σ and
σb. Again, the accurate estimation of σd appears to be problematic whenever the true
parameter value lies close to zero.

While slight dierences are observed in the estimates of σa and σd, the approaches

We proceed to the examination and comparison of condence intervals for the prod-
uct contrasts. Recall that [Brockho et al., 2015] introduced the method described in
Section 2.3 to construct appropriately asymmetric condence intervals for the MAM
that emulate the behavior of the likelihood based condence intervals of the multi-
plicative mixed model. Table 6.3 shows the coverage probabilities for the estimated
95%-condence intervals of both approaches, which is the proportion of the time that
the condence interval contains the true contrast.
For convenience, let MAM and
TMB (in italic) abbreviate the approaches/methods used to obtain the condence
intervals for the MAM and the multiplicative mixed model, respectively.
As seen,
MAM produces quite decent condence intervals, although slightly too wide for small
contrasts and a bit too narrow for large contrasts. TMB also provides very decent
coverage probabilities, but now the coverage for large contrasts is even lower, yield-
ing coverage probabilities as low as 92%. The coverage probabilities derived from the
2000 data sets are quite satisfactory, but the systematic "misbehavior" of the coverage
probabilities is a concern.

To nd an explanation for the systematic misbehaviour of the coverage probabilities,
consider the χ2-test statistic
χ2
jj′ = −2 log
 
Lp(δjj′; y)
Lp(ˆδjj′; y)
!
,
where ˆδjj′ = ˆνj −ˆνj′. According to theory, these test statistics are asymptotically
χ2
1-distributed under H0, when the normality assumption of the maximum likelihood
estimator is met, where H0 is
H0 : νj −νj′ = δjj′,
for j ̸= j′.
==PAGE:76==
64
Simulation Study
Figure 6.2, shows the QQ-plots of the sampled χ2-test statistics where δjj′ is the
true known contrast between product j and j′. The test statistics are computed by
initially deriving the prole likelihood function, Lp(δ; y), for a given contrast and
subsequently computing the log-likelihood ratio between the estimated contrast and
the true contrast. The prole likelihood function is constructed by a cubic spline, tted
to the set of points from which the prole likelihood curve was obtained. Since we
know that H0 is true, when δjj′ is set to the true known contrast, we would expect the
χ2-test statistics to be approximately χ2
1-distributed, unless the normality assumption
is violated. The χ2-test statistics appear to be approximately χ2
1-distributed for small
contrasts, as seen in Figure 6.2.
The sampled test statistics of the large contrasts, however, appear not to follow this
distribution, since they are misaligned with the solid red line that marks the situation
where the theoretical and sampled quantiles coincide.
Take P1-P2 as an example;
the theoretical 95% quantile of the χ2
1-distribution is 3.84, whereas the sampled 95%
quantile is 4.62, which thus leads to too many rejections of the H0 hypothesis, resulting
in too narrow condence intervals.
The χ2-test statistics do not follow a χ2
1-distribution suciently close under H0 for
large contrast. An explanation for this might be that the maximum likelihood esti-
mator is too far from being normally distributed around the true parameter values.
This is not very surprising, knowing that the maximum likelihood estimator for the
variance is prone to bias, which was seen by Table 6.2 and further conrmed in Figure
6.1.

To examine the inuence of the biased variance components, consider the variance for
the product contrasts between product j and j′
Var(¯y.j. −¯y.j′.) = 1
I σ2
b(νj −νj′)2 + 2
I σ2
d +
2
KI σ2.
(6.1)
For the six large contrasts, the variance is primary dictated by the scaling term due to
the multiplication with the squared product contrast. For large contrasts, the small
downward bias of ˆσb becomes more inuential/noticeable, and leads to an underesti-
mation of contrast variance. As a result, the condence intervals become slightly too
narrow, which explains the low coverage probability for the large contrasts. Conversely,
the upward bias of ˆσd appears to have the opposite eect on the coverage probabilities,
which for the small contrasts become noticeable as the scaling term becomes negligible.
As a result of the upward bias of ˆσd, the contrast variance may have become vaguely
overestimated, which explains why the condence intervals have become just tiny bit
too wide.
To support our claim, we re-conduct the simulation study above, where the vari-
ance components in the Laplace likelihood approximation are no longer considered
unknown, but are instead held constant at their true values. The resulting coverage
probabilities are shown in Table 6.4, and clear improvements are seen - it appears
that the bias of the variance components were in fact inuential. Now the coverage
probabilities of the large contrasts are higher, and the coverage probabilities of the
small contrasts are lower. The QQ-plots shown in Figure 6.3, show that the quan-
tiles of the χ2-test statistic are more closely aligned with the theoretical quantiles of
a χ2
1-distribution.

Aside from the bias of the ML estimator, note also that the the empirical variance
of σa and σb in Table 6.2 are fairly large in comparison to the parameter estimates
themselves.
Judging from this, it appears that seven assessors and four replicates
might not provide sucient information to properly estimate σa and σb - an issue that
naturally relates to the problem of bias, since bias becomes more pronounced with low
quantities of data. Since the true variance components are of course unknown, we seek
to overcome the impact of bias in a less far fetched setting - using more data. For this
purpose, 2000 additional data sets are generated consisting of 8 repeated measures of
==PAGE:82==
70
Simulation Study
5 products for each of 20 assessors. As the amount of data increases, the bias of the
variance components is diminished
[ˆσ, ˆΨ]T −[σ, Ψ]T →0 for n →∞,
(6.2)
if the ML estimator is consistent. Before proceeding to the 2000 data sets, we wish to
empirically prove the consistency of the ML estimator through simulation, using 10
sets of simulated data containing the following number of replicates and assessors

sampled test statistics are now more closely aligned with the theoretical quantiles of
a χ2

Chapter 7
Bang & Olufsen - Colour Balance
In this chapter the mixed assessor model as well as the multiplicative mixed model
are used to analyze a supplementary data set acquired from the SensMixed-package
in R, [Kuznetsova et al., 2014b], which consists of sensory evaluations of a series of
Bang & Olufsen (BO) televisions. The purpose of this additional analysis is to assess
model performance in settings, where products fall into more than two categories,
which allows us to model the potential disagreement eect.
All R-code related to this chapter is found in Appendix B, E.15, E.16, E.17, E.18, E.9,
E.13 and E.10.
We commence with a short introduction to data and present as well an exploratory
analysis to summarize the characteristics of data. Subsequently, we conduct a test of
signicance to determine the signicance of the various model eects. We then esti-
mate, examine and compare the model parameters of both models and draw inference
about the products by studying the condence intervals for the product contrasts. As
with the analysis of the food data, we provide a brief simulation study of 1000 simulated
data sets.
7.1
Exploratory Analysis
The products (televisions) are assessed based on numerous attributes such as colour
saturation, colour balance, noise, depth, sharpness, light level etc. We restrict ourselves
to one single attribute - namely colour balance.
The original data set contains only three dierent products, each crossed with an ad-
ditional factor of four levels. We combine these factors to yield one single product
factor with 12 levels to help ensure that the products fall into more than two groups.
==PAGE:88==
76
Bang & Olufsen - Colour Balance
The modied data set thus contains the sensory evaluations of 12 products (J = 12),
where each product was assessed by a panel of eight assessors (I = 8) in two replicates
(K = 2). As in the previous analysis, it is assumed that replications are included in
the random sequence of assessments, such that no block eect is required in the model.
Table 7.1 shows a small extract of the data set, which contain three factors, Assessor,
Product and Replicate, and y denotes the product score.

To test the signicance of the dierent eects by the likelihood based method, consider
the nested sequence of models
M1 : yijk = ai + νj + bi(νj −¯ν) + dij + ϵijk,
M2 : yijk = ai + νj + bi(νj −¯ν) + ϵijk,
M3 : yijk = ai + νj + dij + ϵijk,
M4 : yijk = νj + bi(νj −¯ν) + dij + ϵijk,
M5 : yijk = νj + ϵijk,
M6 : yijk = µ + ϵijk.
The negative log-likelihood of each model are shown in Table 7.3, and these quantities
are used to derive the χ2-test statistics. The results of these tests are summarized in
Table 7.4, which shows that all eects are signicant. While this is consistent with
Table 7.2, all model eects are deemed more signicant by the log-likelihood ratio
tests.

Table 7.9 shows the product eect estimates, sorted in increasing order to more easily
clarify the dierence in the products estimates for the two models. As seen, the order
of the products are not identical. Consider product 10 as an example. This product
has the second largest eect in the multiplicative mixed model, but it only has the
fourth largest eect in the MAM.
Another purpose of this table is to uncover groupings among products and to clarify
which products that are signicantly dierent from others; products not sharing a letter
are signicantly dierent. An additional letter is necessary to describe the groupings
for the multiplicative model. For the sake of convenience, let MAM and TMB (in
italic) abbreviate the analysis of data using the MAM and the multiplicative mixed
model, respectively. According to MAM, product 2 and 11 are given signicantly lower
scores than the remaining products. From the results of TMB, however, these products
are not signicantly dierent from product 5 and 8. In the opposite end of the scale,
TMB assigns an eect to product 4 that is signicantly higher than 7 of the products,
whereas product 4 in MAM has a signicantly higher eect than only 5 products.
This is an important result, as it shows that the two models do not lead to the same
inference about the product contrasts. The tables containing the condence intervals
of the product contrasts for both models are found in Appendix D.

7.3
Simulation Study
The objective of the simulation study presented here is to evaluate the performance
of the multiplicative mixed model and the mixed assessor model. To accomplish this,
1000 data sets are generated using the same set up as the colour balance data set.
Each data set thus consists of 2 repeated assessments of 12 products from each of 8
assessors. The data sets are simulated according to the multiplicative mixed model,
where the true product eects and the variance components are set to the parameter
estimates from Table 7.7.
As a rst, the MAM is applied to each data set to derive the average parameter
estimates presented in Table 7.10. As seen, the average parameter estimates lie very
close to the true parameter values, and no conspicuous bias seems to be present.
