Chapter 8
Fitting the mixed two-way ANOVA
Model to the Food Data
In the beginning of Chapter 2, the classic mixed two-way ANOVA model was shortly
described.
yijk = µ + ai + νj + gij + ϵijk,
ai ∼i.i.d. N(0, σ2
a),
gij ∼i.i.d. N(0, σ2
g),
ϵijk ∼i.i.d. N(0, σ2).
As mentioned, this is a frequently used model for the analysis of sensory data. In this
chapter, this model is used for the analysis of the food data to draw inference about
the product contrasts. Following this, we conduct a short simulation study to nd the
coverage probabilities of the 95%-condence intervals for the contrasts, estimated by
this classic method. The results are then compared to the results obtained from the
previous analyses, in which the mixed assessor model and the multiplicative mixed
model were used.
The R-code related to this chapter is found in Appendix E.19.
The model is tted to the data by the function, lmer, from the R-package lme4, [Bates
et al., 2014]. This gives the estimates shown in Table 8.1. The product eect estimates
are equal to the those of the MAM, for the simple reason that they correspond to the
raw product averages, y.j., when data is balanced. The estimate of σ is found directly
by taking the square-root of the MSE, and this estimate is therefore equal to that of
the MAM as well. On the other hand, ˆσa is smaller than in the previous analyses,
for what reason the random eect coecients, ai, are numerically lower as well. The
estimated variance for the interaction eect is, in turn, much bigger than the combined
variance for the scaling and the disagreement eect in the multiplicative model.

8.1
Simulation Study
The mixed two-way ANOVA model is now tted to the 2000 simulated data sets from
Chapter 6, containing the assessments of 5 products from 7 assessors in 4 replicates.
Table 8.3 shows the coverage probabilities of the 95%-condence intervals for the
product contrasts, found by the aforementioned classic method.
We see that the
probability of covering the true contrast is too high for small contrasts and way too
low for large contrasts. We know that the actual variance of the contrast increases
with the size of the contrast itself, and therefore it is not reasonable to have condence
intervals of equal width for dierent contrast sizes. The consequence of having intervals
with equal width is that the intervals are too wide for small contrasts and too narrow
for large contrasts, which makes the coverage probability too high for small contrasts
and too low for large contrasts.

==PAGE:103==
Chapter 9
Discussion
In this chapter we elaborate on our results and we discuss some of the model assump-
tions followed by suggestions for future work in the end of the chapter.
All code related to this chapter is found in Appendix E.20, E.21 and E.22.
Condence intervals for the product contrasts
For convenience, let MAM and TMB (in italic) abbreviate the approaches/methods
used to obtain the condence intervals for either model. Both approaches produce
satisfactory condence intervals for the product contrasts, which in the food data led
to the same overall inference about the products. In the simulation study in Chapter
6, the bias of the maximum likelihood estimator appears to contribute to the sys-
tematic misbehaviour of the condence intervals for the multiplicative mixed model,
which for large contrasts become too narrow and slightly too wide for small contrasts,
thus aecting the coverage probabilities. Our explanation is that the estimates of the
variance components, as a result of bias, are not normally distributed around the true
parameter values, for what reason the distribution of the sampled χ2-test statistics of
the log-likelihood ratio of the true and the estimated contrasts does not suciently
approximate a χ2
1-distribution.
To understand why the inuence of bias is greater
for large contrasts, we examine the variance expression of the product contrasts. It
appears that the scaling term,
1
I σ2
b(νj −νj′)2, contributes to the underestimation of
the contrast variance, whenever the estimate of σb is biased downwards, and conse-
quently the underestimation becomes more substantial for large contrasts due to the
squared product contrast, (νj −νj′)2. Similarly for small contrasts, there is indication
of slight overestimation of the contrast variance due to the upward bias in ˆσd, which
as a result gives rise to slightly too wide condence intervals. We support these claims
by re-analyzing the simulated data, where the variance components in the Laplace
likelihood approximation are no longer considered unknown, but rather held constant
at their true values. The intention is to evaluate the coverage probabilities in a setting,
==PAGE:104==
92
Discussion
where no bias is present. Without the inuence of downward and upward bias in ˆσb
and ˆσd, respectively, the contrast variance is not systematically over- or underesti-
mated, and as such leads to a reasonable increase in the coverage probabilities of large
contrasts, and a reasonable decrease in the coverage probabilities for small contrasts.
In line with this, 2000 additional data sets were simulated containing the sensory
evaluations of 5 products given by 20 assessors in 8 replicates, so as to create a set-
ting in which more data is available in order to reduce bias. Also here the coverage
probabilities showed signs of improvements.
As in the analysis of the food data, both MAM and TMB produce satisfactory con-
dence intervals for the product contrasts in the analysis of colour balance. The two
approaches did, however, not lead to the same statistical inference about the products.
The order of the product eects and the inference about the contrasts were shown to
dier slightly between the models. The analysis of the multiplicative mixed model
results in slightly too low coverage probabilities for this data, and the downward bias
in the estimated variance components seems, again, to be the root cause of this. In
this study however, there appears to be no obvious systematic misbehavior of the con-
dence intervals. One plausible explanation is that the product contrasts in the colour
balance data are in general lower than in the food data; the largest contrast in colour
balance is 3.77 compared to 6.76 in the food data, and as such the squared contrast
in the variance expression makes the downward bias in ˆσb much less inuential in the
colour balance data.
As we believe that the bias of our maximum likelihood estimated variance components
causes the misbehaviour of the estimated condence intervals, it could be interesting
to explore the possibility of combining the Laplace method with a restricted maximum
likelihood approach so as to avoid the bias. We leave this for future work.
The two-product-group setting
The obvious "two-product-group" setting in the food data renders both models unable
to model the disagreement eect. Nonetheless, we have chosen to analyze this data
such that we can compare the results with the results in [Brockho et al., 2015]. Sec-
ondly, the "low" number of products, assessors and replicates, facilitates simulation
studies; despite the high computation speed of the TMB approach, the number of op-
timizations used to obtain the prole likelihoods for each contrast, multiplied by the
number of data sets, have shown to be highly time consuming. This explains why the
simulation study of the food data includes twice as many data sets as in the second
simulation study of the color balance data.
For the color balance data, the prole
likelihood for each of 66 product contrasts uses roughly 35 optimizations, a number
which by default in the TMB is three time as large. Despite this reduction, the prole
likelihoods of all contrasts for one single data set require all together roughly 2500
optimizations, and it takes nearly 4 days to analyze all 1000 data sets for the colour
balance study.
==PAGE:105==
93
The implementations
Our early implementation of the Laplace likelihood proved to be computationally ex-
haustive.
One large contributor to the computation time is the derivation of the
Hessian of the Laplace likelihood, which more than doubled the computation time.
The Hessian is computed subsequent to the optimization of the Laplace likelihood by
en external call to the function, hessian, from the R-package numDeriv, [Gilbert and
Varadhan, 2012], which takes as input the Laplace likelihood function as well as the
vector of model parameters, [ν, log(σ), log(Ψ)]T . The nlminb function used for both
the inner and outer optimization routine does not return the Hessian at optimum, and
so to circumvent the expensive derivation of the Hessian, one could consider imple-
menting the Quasi-Newton BFGS algorithm so that the Hessian becomes accessible.
Recall that [Christensen and Brockho, 2012] suggests a few ways to increase compu-
tation speed such as supplying the true gradient of the joint log-likelihood with respect
to the random eects, supplying the analytical Jacobian of the model function with
respect to the random eects, and lastly, proling out the residual variance compo-
nent, σ, of the Laplace likelihood function. As we believe that the speed improvements
will not be sucient as well as to avoid the heavy programming burden, we chose not
to pursue these speed improvements, but instead considered automatic dierentiation
and the TMB-package.
The mixed two-way ANOVA model
In Chapter 8 the classic two-way ANOVA model was tted to the food data, and
the results were compared to results obtained by both the MAM and the multiplica-
tive mixed model. Under the assumption that sensory data is best described by the
multiplicative mixed model, the two-way ANOVA model produces inappropriate 95%-
condence intervals, with coverage probabilities as low as 84% and as high as 99, 9%.
In the multiplicative mixed model the contrast variance depends on the size of the
contrast, but the two-way model does not account for this, and as such produces sym-
metric condence intervals of equal widths for all product contrasts. This explains
the poor coverage probabilities. It is also important to note that when the condence
intervals for small contrasts are too wide, the risk of failing to detect small product
dierences increases.
As stated in Section 2, the denominator in the F-test, when testing the signicance of
products, is MSInteraction in the mixed two-way model while it is MSDisagreement in
the MAM. As a consequence, the MAM has greater power to detect product dierences
than the two-way model, if a scaling eect is present. This is illustrated in the mixed
two-way ANOVA table for the colour balance data, shown in Table 9.1. The product
eect is still very signicant, but the p-value has increased from 3.88e-06 (Table 7.2)
to 2.02e-05. Table 7.4 showed that the p-value for the multiplicative mixed model is
3.96e-08, which indicates that the power to detect product dierences is even greater
for this model.
The poor coverage probabilities and the lower power to detect product dierences for
the mixed two-way ANOVA model emphasize the potential improvements of applying
==PAGE:106==
94
Discussion
more complicated models, such as the mixed multiplicative model, where the assessor
scale range dierences are taken into account.
Table 9.1: ANOVA table for the colour balance data.

SS

2.58
Block eect
Throughout this project, it was assumed that the replications were included in the
random sequence of assessments, such that no block eect was required in the model.
With a block eect, ck, the multiplicative mixed model is formulated as:
yijk = ai + ck + νj + bi(νj −¯ν) + dij + ϵijk,
(9.1)
ai ∼i.i.d. N(0, σ2
a),
ck ∼i.i.d. N(0, σ2

The χ2-test statistic of the log-likelihood ratio of the standard multiplicative mixed

which leads to a p-value of 0.0463 under the H0 hypothesis of no block eect. Since
the block eect is signicant at α = 0.05, our assumption of replicates being included
in the random sequence of assessments does not appear to hold.
The inclusion of a block eect changes the structure of the precision matrix of the joint
distribution of data and the random eects. Figure 9.1 shows the precision matrix and
the inverse precision matrix, and as a result of the block eect, the latter has become
more dense. The upper left square in Figure 9.1b is the covariance matrix of data.
The inverse covariance matrix is used in the closed-form expression of the marginal
likelihood, and this matrix is fully dense as seen in Figure 9.2b. In Section 5.3 it was
argued that the rather sparse structure of the inverted covariance matrix in Figure
5.5b might possibly allow for ecient optimization of the marginal likelihood directly.
By the inclusion of a block eect, however, observations from dierent assessors are
no longer independent for what reason the inverted covariance matrix has become
dense - in this case, fully dense. This illustrates the benet of optimizing the Laplace
likelihood function via TMB, which utilizes the sparsity of the precision matrix.
We wish to highlight the exibility of TMB; the inclusion of a block eect in the mul-
tiplicative mixed model is easily handled with TMB, and requires only an additional
few lines of code.

Assessor specic error variance and weighted scores
The exploratory analysis of the food data revealed varying assessor inconsistencies i.e.
varying individual error variances. The multiplicative mixed model assumes constant
σ for all assessors, and may as such seem too restrictive in this regard. [Brockho
and Skovgaard, 1994] introduces the following assessor-model for dierent assessor
variances,
yijk = µ + αi + νj + βiνj + ϵijk,
ϵijk ∼i.i.d. N(0, σ2
i ),
with PI
i=1 βi = 0. Note that all of the eects are considered xed in this model, and
that only the scaling part of the interaction is included.
With the eect of assessor considered xed, this is a useful model for the evaluation of
assessor performance. At rst sight, the individual variances seem to be appropriate
measures of assessor performance, since a low variance signies consistency in the
scores given by the assessor. This measure would, however, favour assessors scoring
consistently within a narrow range, whether or not they are able to actually separate
the products. A consistent assessor that is not able to separate the products, do not
only have a low individual variance, but also a low scaling coecient. This means that
a more appropriate measure of assessor performance is βi/σi. The square of this term
is used as a weight in the calculation of the product eects, such that scores given by
an assessor with a low value of βi/σi gets a low weight in the calculation.
The mixed assessor model and the multiplicative mixed model might be improved by
==PAGE:109==
97
allowing for individual variance parameters, but as [Brockho et al., 2015] states, the
analyses of product eects are based on the average levels of assessor error variance,
and statistical analysis of product dierences is thus not expected to be very sensitive
to such a model mis-specication.
As it turns out, however, the multiplicative mixed model does in fact incorporate an
assessor dependent weighting of the scores during the computation of the product
eects. To realize this, consider the following model
yijk = µ + αi + βiνj + dij + ϵijk,
(9.3)
dij ∼N(0, σ2
d),
ϵijk ∼N(0, σ2)
which only diers from our multiplicative mixed model by considering the assessor
eect as xed. In the above model, the parameters in the multiplicative term may be
regarded as the scores and loadings of a one-component Principal Component Analysis
(PCA) on the product-by-assessor matrix of observations (averaged over replicates).
The estimates of νj and βi correspond to the scores and the loadings, respectively. In
other words, the product eect estimates, ˆνj, is a weighted average,
ˆνj = λ1¯y1j. + λ2¯y2j. + · · · + λI ¯yIj. ,
(9.4)
where the weights, λi, are the assessor loadings from the PCA. These loadings/weights
depend on the scaling coecients of the assessors, meaning that scores given by an
assessor with a high level of scaling are weighted higher than scores given by an assessor
with a low scaling coecient.
Our multiplicative mixed model diers as mentioned from this model by considering
the assessor main eect and the scaling eect as random.
This indicates that the
maximum likelihood estimates of νj may be a bit dierent from the estimates obtained
from model 9.3, but may still share some of the aforementioned weighting properties.
To investigate this further, a PCA on the product-by-assessor matrix for the colour
balance data is conducted. Table 9.3 and 9.4 show the loadings and the scores for the
rst principal component (PC1). As seen, the loadings for assessor 5, 7 and 8 have
the highest values, which conforms with Table 7.8, which shows that these 3 assessors
have the largest estimated scaling coecients. Table 7.7 shows the estimated product
eects for the multiplicative mixed model, with product 2 and 11 in the bottom of the
scale and with product 4 and 10 in the top of the scale. This is consistent with the
scores of PC1, which shows that product 2 and 11 have the most negative scores and
that product 4 and 10 have the most positive scores.
If the products are ordered according to their PCA scores, we get the sequence in Table
9.5. We see that the order is very similar to the order obtained by the multiplicative
mixed model - the only dierence is that product 2 is listed before product 11 and that
product 1 comes ahead of product 3 (Table 7.9). Comparing the order in Table 9.5
with the order obtained by the MAM, the dierence is clearly bigger. This suggests
that the maximum likelihood estimates of νj in the multiplicative mixed model do in
fact share some of the "PCA-properties" of model 9.3, where the νjs are calculated as
weighted averages. This explains why the product eect estimates of the MAM are
dierent, as these are simple product averages and not weighted averages.

4
It could be interesting to nd out whether allowing for individual variances would
change the weights, such that they depend not only on the scaling coecients but also
the individual variances. Another point of interest is to examine if scaling depended
weights are even appropriate. In [Smith et al., 2003] the multiplicative mixed model
is considered, where the heterogeneity of the error variance between assessors is ac-
commodated. The main purpose of the analysis in the paper is, however, to examine
assessor performance, and the assessor eect is therefore assumed to be xed while the
products are considered random. They evaluate the assessor performance by calculat-
ing a so called discriminating ability measure, which is the ratio between the assessor
specic product variance and the individual error variance - the higher the ratio, the
better the discriminating ability. The individual error variance and the assessor spe-
cic product variance are estimated in a way that corresponds to performing ANOVAs
for each assessors. The paper argues that this measure for assessor reliability is more
appropriate than the measure from [Brockho and Skovgaard, 1994], which is the ratio
between the assessor specic scaling coecient and the individual error variance. The
argumentation is based on the fact that an assessor only obtains a high scaling coef-
cient if he/she agrees with the panel as a whole, which means that an assessor that
are actually able to distinguish between the products gets a low performance measure
if he/she disagrees with the overall product pattern.
This opens up a discussion on how the scores should be weighted in the calculation of
the product eects, for optimal inference about product dierences. Perhaps a weight
based on the scaling coecient is not the best choice, but we leave this for future work.
Normality assumption of data
In the multiplicative mixed model it is assumed that data is normally distributed.
This assumption is of course inappropriate to some degree, since the response is al-
ways conned to a predened scale in sensory data. Alternatively, the observations
could be analyzed as ordered categorical data, also known as ordinal data, implying
that the scores are classied in predened categories. This is a common practice in
==PAGE:111==
9.1 Future Work
99
the eld of sensometrics, where data is based on human assessments. Considering the
observations on an ordinal scale, the most popular regression model is the cumulative
link model that treats the response as categorical and avoids predictions outside the
dened scale, [Christensen, 2015b].
It could be interesting to analyse consumer data by applying a cumulative link model
and compare the inference with the inference obtained by tting the multiplicative
mixed model to data. This can be done by use of the R-package Ordinal, [Christensen,
2015a], which provides functionalities to analyse ordinal data by tting cumulative
link models.
9.1
Future Work
Our proposals for future work are:
 to consider a restricted maximum likelihood (REML) approach to avoid the bias
on the estimated variance components. This should be doable since the xed
eects enter linearly in the multiplicative mixed model, [Pinheiro and Bates,
2000].
 to let the multiplicative mixed model allow for assessor specic error variance.
 to include block eects in the multiplicative mixed model.
 to consider the data as ordinal and apply a cumulative link model.
==PAGE:112==
100
Discussion
==PAGE:113==
Chapter 10
Conclusion
The key aim of this project was to consider the multiplicative mixed model in its
genuine form, as an alternative to its linear approximation, the Mixed Assessor Model
(MAM). Our objective was to eciently implement the Laplace approximation to the
marginal likelihood so as to estimate the multiplicative mixed model as well as to
uncover the benets and limitations of a full likelihood approach. Our study involved
a comparison of the multiplicative mixed model and the approximate MAM in terms
of dierences in parameter estimates and statistical inference obtained by either ap-
proach.
As a rst, the ANOVA table of the MAM and the log-likelihood ratio tests for the
multiplicative mixed model led to the same overall conclusion about the signicance
of the model eects. From these tests, the multiplicative mixed model appeared to
have higher power to detect product dierences than the MAM, yet both these models
have higher power than the classic two-way mixed ANOVA model.
The product eect estimates of the two models showed great resemblance, although
slight dierences were observed in the estimated variance components. The variance
components of the multiplicative mixed model received lower estimates, and the sim-
ulation studies revealed that these low estimates were attributable to the known bias
of the maximum likelihood estimator. Despite the similar parameter estimates, our
study showed that the two models do not always lead to the same inference about
product contrasts.
As a result of bias, the 95%-condence interval for the product contrasts of the multi-
plicative mixed model became too narrow, naturally aecting the 95%-coverage prob-
abilities, which for the particular data considered, ranged from roughly 90% to 96%.
Furthermore, the bias appeared to lead to an underestimation of the contrast vari-
ance, which increased with the size of the contrast. Consequently, the bias produced a
systematic misbehaviour in the width of the condence intervals, such that the degree
to which the coverage probability of a given contrast deviated from 95% depended on
==PAGE:114==
102
Conclusion
the size of the contrast. We showed that the inuence of bias may be reduced by an
increase in the quantity of data.
The novel method proposed in [Brockho et al., 2015], for the estimation of the 95%-
condence intervals for the product contrasts, produced appropriate condence inter-
vals by taking into account that the contrast variance increases as the absolute size of
the contrast increases. This is a feature not shared by the standard approach, for the
estimation of condence intervals, in the classic two-way mixed ANOVA model. The
proposed method proved to compute asymmetric condence intervals that reasonably
emulated the behaviour of the condence intervals for the multiplicative mixed model.
The condence intervals performed very well, yielding coverage probabilities ranging
from 0.91 to 0.96, with the intervals being slightly too narrow for large contrasts and
slightly too wide for small contrasts.
Our early Laplace implementation had shown to be computationally exhaustive, to
such an extend that simulation studies would be somewhat impossible to conduct
within reasonable time. Automatic dierentiation of the joint log-likelihood oered by
TMB increased the computation speed immensely, and the time spend to optimize the
Laplace likelihood decreased to a matter of seconds. This showed that care must be
taken in implementing the Laplace method, even for models containing only a modest
number of random eects.
To summarize, we have shown that it is possible to estimate the multiplicative mixed
model by a full likelihood approach. Overall, the statistical inference drawn from ei-
ther model is quite similar, and it is hard to nd the improvements obtained by the
full likelihood approach, since the novel method proposed by [Brockho et al., 2015]
produced satisfactory condence intervals for the product contrasts, which slightly su-
perseded the likelihood-based condence intervals of the multiplicative mixed model.
One benet of the likelihood approach is, however, that the uncertainty of the param-
eter estimates is easily obtained, which is not the case for the MAM.

the contrast between product j1 and product j2.

In this double for-loop, we go through all of the product combinations and compute
the t-statistic and the degrees of freedom for all of the δ-values in the grid from before.
This enables us to nd out which of the delta-values that belong to the 95%-condence
interval, and from this the condence limits are computed. Further, the p-values for
the contrasts are estimated.

((1-c)*MS.dis)^2/((nA -1)*(nP -2)) )

Appendix C
Identiability
The equations from (3.9) solved in Maple. In the code, a denotes σa, b denotes σb, d
denotes σd and s denotes σ. Further, p1 = ν1 −ν and p2 = ν2 −ν.
>
with(LinearAlgebra):
>
L1 :=a1^2+p1^2*b1^2+d1^2+s1^2-(a2^2+p1^2*b2^2+d2^2+s2^2)=0:
>
L2 :=a1^2+p1^2*b1^2+d1^2-(a2^2+p1^2*b2^2+d2^2)=0:
>
L3 :=a1^2+p2^2*b1^2+d1^2+s1^2-(a2^2+p2^2*b2^2+d2^2+s2^2)=0:
>
L4 :=a1^2+p2^2*b1^2+d1^2-(a2^2+p2^2*b2^2+d2^2)=0:
>
L5 :=a1^2+p1*p2*b1^2-(a2^2+p1*p2*b2^2)=0:
>
solve([L1,L2,L3,L4,L5],{s1,s2,a1,a2,b1,b2,d1,d2});
{a1 = a2, a2 = a2, b1 = b2, b2 = b2, d1 = d2, d2 = d2, s1 = s2, s2 = s2} ,
{a1 = −a2, a2 = a2, b1 = b2, b2 = b2, d1 = d2, d2 = d2, s1 = s2, s2 = s2} ,
{a1 = a2, a2 = a2, b1 = −b2, b2 = b2, d1 = d2, d2 = d2, s1 = s2, s2 = s2} ,
{a1 = −a2, a2 = a2, b1 = −b2, b2 = b2, d1 = d2, d2 = d2, s1 = s2, s2 = s2} ,
{a1 = a2, a2 = a2, b1 = b2, b2 = b2, d1 = d2, d2 = d2, s1 = −s2, s2 = s2} ,
{a1 = −a2, a2 = a2, b1 = b2, b2 = b2, d1 = d2, d2 = d2, s1 = −s2, s2 = s2} ,
{a1 = a2, a2 = a2, b1 = −b2, b2 = b2, d1 = d2, d2 = d2, s1 = −s2, s2 = s2} ,
{a1 = −a2, a2 = a2, b1 = −b2, b2 = b2, d1 = d2, d2 = d2, s1 = −s2, s2 = s2} ,
{a1 = a2, a2 = a2, b1 = b2, b2 = b2, d1 = −d2, d2 = d2, s1 = s2, s2 = s2} ,
{a1 = −a2, a2 = a2, b1 = b2, b2 = b2, d1 = −d2, d2 = d2, s1 = s2, s2 = s2} ,
{a1 = a2, a2 = a2, b1 = −b2, b2 = b2, d1 = −d2, d2 = d2, s1 = s2, s2 = s2} ,
{a1 = −a2, a2 = a2, b1 = −b2, b2 = b2, d1 = −d2, d2 = d2, s1 = s2, s2 = s2} ,
{a1 = a2, a2 = a2, b1 = b2, b2 = b2, d1 = −d2, d2 = d2, s1 = −s2, s2 = s2} ,
{a1 = −a2, a2 = a2, b1 = b2, b2 = b2, d1 = −d2, d2 = d2, s1 = −s2, s2 = s2} ,

fitLA = function(data , stats) {
4

= data$Assessor;

grad.Ran = function(nu , w, sigma , sigma.a, sigma.b, sigma.d) {
14
a = w[1: nA];
15
b = w[(nA+1) :(nA *2) ];
16
d = w[(nA *2+1) :(nA*2+nA*nP)];
17
M = (nu[Product]-mean(nu));
18
19
gradA
= by(-(y - a[Assessor] - nu[Product] - b[Assessor ]*M
20
- d[ AssessorProduct ])/sigma ^2, Assessor , sum) + a/sigma.a^2;
21
gradB
= by(-(y - a[Assessor] - nu[Product] - b[Assessor ]*M
22
- d[ AssessorProduct ])*M/sigma ^2, Assessor , sum)+b/sigma.b^2;
23
gradD
= by(-(y - a[Assessor] - nu[Product] - b[Assessor ]*M
24
- d[ AssessorProduct ])/sigma ^2, AssessorProduct ,sum)
25
+ d/sigma.d^2;
26
gradRan = c(gradA , gradB , gradD)
27
}

l.LA = function(theta) {

fitX2 = function(s) {
275
t1 = qchisq(p = c(0.10 ,
0.6) , df = 1);
276
t2 = quantile(y, c(0.10 ,
0.6))
277
slope = diff(t2)/diff(t1);
278
int = t2[1] - slope * t1 [1]
279
return(int + s*slope)
280
}

56
nll
-= dnorm(d, Type (0), sigma_D , true).sum ();
57
58
return
nll;
59
}
